Chapter XIII.
On the Restoration of Fractures of the Wrist and Fingers.
Know that at the wrist of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers there may be a fracture, and do not injure them but rub them gently.
And when a fracture or dislocation occurs in the hand, ensure that the patient remains lying down and keep the limb straight for about one hundred equal parts of time, then place your hand over the extended limb.
And then extend the broken bones and the doctor should align them with good reduction.
And then apply plaster and cotton, if no abscess forms use a cautery instead; then place a candle above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen.
More so if the rupture is in the interior parts of the palm, then make a semblance of a bunch of linen, and command the patient to hold that tightly with his ruptured palm; then have him hold it tightly with linen long.
And let the candle be held in linen which is light, so that the wax is applied to the palm; and hold it tightly as needed.
If the fracture is on the outer parts, then place another strip of cloth above the bone and another on the part, in your palm, so that your hand is open and straight; then place the bandage, as if you were turning your hand at a right angle between your fingers with grains of wheat.
If you accidentally fracture one of the bones in the fingers, then if it is the finger tip, equalize it according to its position and then strengthen it on the palm. If you wish, place a small strip above the right side so that the fracture is elevated and not displaced.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, as in the middle finger, or the index, or the ring finger, then equalize and bind it to the finger where the fracture is; or bind all of them according to their continuity, which is better; or place a small strip above the fracture, as we have said for the tip.
And consider, in your restoration, and nearby, the placement of cautery and opposition—during your operation—according to what is indicated when such a cause occurs, as detailed in the narration.
Chapter XIV.
For the restoration of fracture of the rib bone.
The rib bone, when fractured, is felt according to where it is displaced forward and backward.
And the restoration is done by strengthening the ligament over the fracture and placing another ligament below the fracture. The patient should be laid on his side and face downward.
And then the physician extends the ligament along its proper part according to the quality of the fracture, when the break is in the middle of the rib bone.
But if it is applied to the angle of the rib, then place the ligament along the groove of the lower part, so that the extension reaches there, and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is applied to the groove, then place the ligament along the groove, and the extension to the parts below.
And then the doctor aligns the bone by bringing his hands close to where you have reduced it according to natural form; the bone is joined by good articulation; then apply a plaster and a bandage, so as not to cause an abscess in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you let it be for some days until the abscess is ready to be cauterized, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made as follows: first ensure you wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and tie off any excess; then wrap the bandage until it reaches the ankle and the back of the thigh.
And insert a long needle between the thigh and the bandage near the fracture at the breaks, ensuring the ends of the needle extend well above both parts, then wrap the excess bandage around the bandage and thigh.
And then place something over the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place a stick over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the space between the waist and thigh with many towels, so that the tension is constricted.
And then begin with a strong bandage around the middle over the location of the fracture using three or four wraps. The closer you are to the site of the fracture, the tighter, more firm, and less soft your bandage should be.
And then extend the ends of the bandage to the parts where it reaches the foot's cavity, and tighten both ends of the stakes on the other side so that the plaster does not shift from its place.
And then place the bandage over it so that it does not cause corrosion, abscess, or inflammation and resembles those; if any of these conditions occur due to some cause, then apply the appropriate remedy, and correct whatever occurs promptly, as we have said previously.
If the bone is badly damaged, then try to equalize it as much as possible; if not, set it and immobilize it, and care for that which was preceded by the recollection until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is strengthened by another that is adjusted to it with stays, as we have said in the assistant and in the arm.

And know that the hip is strengthened in about ten days or a few more weeks, and all this depends on the diversity of the complications and other arrangements.

Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the ribs.

And if you break two bones together, the fracture will be set in all directions.
And if the finer bone is broken, the fracture will be set in front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find the fracture, and it is one of those conditions that is not under your control.
And then administer extension, equalization, and ligature of the tendons accordingly.

Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the ankle and the bones of the foot and toes.
An ankle does not fracture completely; more often fractures occur in the bones of the foot; and fractures rarely occur in the toes of the foot, and do not fracture but are rubbed in the appropriate place.

Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when restored and subsequently allow the delicate member to heal according to its natural course.
When a bone fracture is restored, and subsequently allow the delicate member to heal according to its natural course, this is done for several reasons: one reason is due to the insufficient dissolution of the membrane and the improper healing process; or due to excessive compression of the membranes, hindering nutrition from reaching the member; or due to excessive ointment application; or due to unnecessary movement at inappropriate times; or due to a lack of strength in the body of the patient and weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give the patient something to eat and make his heart tender while the disease multiplies within him; give him baths; and insert hope and joy into him.
And place your hand over the member, so that the hand draws in nourishment, or continue the wet compress with vinegar, allowing the nourishment to flow and return to its natural form.

Chapter XXIII.
On speech concerning dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint in the body, which prohibits movement and distorts the shape of the limb, causing significant pain and injuries.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is important to immediately try to reduce it as soon as possible. If delayed, the area can become infected and make the reduction of the dislocation much more difficult.
And for this reason, one should neither be too tight nor too loose during the infection period, as tight or loose bands can cause spasms and severe pains.
When this occurs, you should immediately perform a flail amputation of the foot, and allow it to rest until the infection subsides; then, apply hot water and oil to the limb, and try to reduce the dislocation with ease.
And each limb should be treated with the remedy of which its location will remind you.
And I have already arranged the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body upwards, then downwards, and thus.
Chapter 24.
For the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, proceed.
When both jaws are dislocated without fracture; and the dislocation of both is caused in one of two ways: either they are slightly moved from their places with a small movement and slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated, during which they are softened at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and cannot have that nor can close their mouth, and their tongue is wrapped in a cloth.
More often, when the dislocation is slight, it returns to its place in major displacements by itself due to minor causes.
More often, if the dislocation is complete and thorough, then you must apply a gentle reduction immediately, and not delay it entirely.
And the minister should hold the patient's head, and insert the doctor with one hand in the cavity of the dislocation inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both his thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands be outside aligned with those; and command the patient to cease moving his jaw and allow those to return from any part; and the doctor align the jaw and enclose the jaw inside as it returns to its place.

And this should be given for easy administration, and you should not trouble the opening of the mouth with eating, drinking, and speaking until the jaw is relaxed and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws when dislocated at any time and cannot be returned to their proper places, then moving and shaking accidents will cause fevers and frequent swelling. And the belly of the patient is greatly swollen, and there is much vomiting of pure bile.

Capitol. xxvi.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.

And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the shoulder of the patient on a table and instruct them to extend their arm. The physician then places his hand or palm over the vicinity of the joint and gently push it back into its proper position.
Moreover, however, it must be considered if the palm is already placed on the abdomen of the hand, then the patient pauses the two fingers of his hand on a table to extend and reduce; and if the dislocation is precedent, place the two fingers of the hand, then the position of the hand of that intrinsically on the table, so that the hand of the doctor falls over that potentiality of the joint.
And so, if it returns quickly, that is good; otherwise, then strengthen that and wrap the abscess, leaving that alone; and do not repeat this, for if you do not, you should neither suffer nor be able to proceed with the reduction of it until days pass.
Moreover, however, the joint is to be treated according to its tortuosity; and do not note any cause to the patient, but rather that the hand is mollified and cannot strengthen any cause; then you know that the nerves are torn or stretched, and then there is no harm in it so long as you strengthen it with a cautery near the site and fortify it with this providing some cause.
And since the gout has returned, then pause over the dressing which we have recommended, and let it be strengthened for five days; and then release it and leave it, and exercise the hand.
And if the movement in it has been obstructed and some gravity occurs in it, then apply that which is cold and with friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the cure of dislocation of fingers.
For some time, reduce the fingers completely.
And when one finger is dislocated on the side of the palm or in the palm itself, hold the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend that part which is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend that part and extend it until it is straightened in that day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that narration, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And make it seem as though you are doing so, if it is dislocated in the belly of the hand, and hold that at that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And make it seem as though you are doing so when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Capitol.
For the cure of dislocation of the spine in the back.
When you encounter any of the vertebrae in the back or neck completely dislocated, and multiple vertebrae are displaced from their places, then there is no cure, for death ensues to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion escapes against their will, and they cannot retain it; many times, the muscles of the patient or their legs or their arms, or one of those, become softened. Or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for multiple vertebrae are displaced and multiple vertebrae cause the removal of those to four parts.
Then that is relaxed at the rear parts and is named jaundice.
May the cure of it be that when you consider whether jaundice arises from biliousness, then in that case there is no cure and neither does it heal completely.
May it be that in those cases where it comes from congestion or percussion or resemblance to those, the Ancients have spoken at length about the methods of cure, detailing which returns most effectively. But I shall now abbreviate what is necessary from their teachings, for my own closeness to understanding allows me to explain this clearly and format it into various instruments from which I will show and expand upon them.
And of those, only jaundice arising in both sides properly as I have described is cured.
And in such a way that the patient is laid out on his back close to the wall, and is spread out so that his feet are well apart.
And place a straight stake upright, inserted into the ground at the head end of the patient, and another stake vertically through the feet of the patient at the other end of the stake.
The minister hold the stake, and it is not fixed in a hole; another minister holds the other according to that form.
And wrap around the head of the patient and tightly bind it with a firm ligature; extend the end of the ligature to the stake which is fixed at the head of the patient, and tie that there; and firmly bind another ligature around the hips of the patient under his armpits, and hold his legs; and adjust all the ligatures and tie those to the other stake which holds the feet of the patient.
And the minister should extend the ligature to the part of the stake, and do not move the two stakes and the ligatures in which they are inserted, but they should not be fixed thus as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or places one hand on a table and adjusts his feet on the table as it returns.
And if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the width or length is about three braccia, and cut into the wall where we said was near the hernia a place at the edge of the table. And then place the majority of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other edge, and compresses well so that the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a turn which is revolving in your hand; and when you stand on the ground, place the patient's head at the end of the fingers of two sticks, the length of each being one braccio, and place the patient's head at the end of the fingers of two sticks similarly; and let the distance between each stick be a palm, and then in each stick thread a cord, and let each be firmly fixed to the ground so that they do not move.
And there should be a round stick for the turn, and it should be rotated in the two grooves of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a groove in which the stick is placed, the length of which is one palm's breadth as it is rotated; and in the other two sticks it is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments you have stretched in the feet of the patient in the groove in which you place its head, and the ligaments you have stretched in the thighs of the patient in the turn in which you place its legs.
And then you should place each turn and have a minister that twist the turn with his hand, and the doctor hold the spine according to what we have said.
And when the spine is returned and the area is held, then cover it with a paste of honey, and about wool; then place a strip of tablecloth over the paste, whose width should be about three fingers or almost that, and its length such that it covers the area of yellow bile, and over the part of the spine.
And then apply the litharge which we have mentioned, and use the patient for subtle nourishment until it is healed.
And if there is any danger at the site where healing ends, then ensure that medicines that moderate and soothe are administered gradually over a long period, which we have recommended; and already a sheet of lead is being administered in that location.
And sometimes accidents occur at the very end of the vertebrae, and are caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone where you already suspect that no suspicion should be allowed to arise about it with this medicine, for it will greatly cause your accident to lead to death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the cure of dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should only occur due to dislocation alone, and do not occur in those cases where other small movements and cavities are involved.
The joint of the ankle is dislocated according to four manners; it is dislocated to the inner parts, or to the outer parts, or to the front parts, or to the rear parts.
It is mostly dislocated to the inner parts, and little to the front or rear parts.
The sign of dislocation to the inner parts is that when you adjust the footband of the healthy side against the injured side, the latter appears longer and the sole more prominent than the healthy one. The injured foot cannot bend its toe; this is the location where you should cauterize the lengonar, so that the head of the swelling is already reached here.
The sign of the one to which dislocation occurs to the outer parts is that the injuries of the foot are contrary to this dislocation.
The sign of the one to which dislocation occurs to the front parts is that you expand the footband according to need, but it cannot bend the toe that is in pain. If forced to go further and it cannot do so toward the front parts; his urine is retained and his swelling is apostemat, and you should treat his ambulation on the sole.
And a sign of dislocation in the parts behind is that the leg does not expand, nor can it bend what should bend, and the thigh is shorter than the other, and the bent part is softened, and the knee is deviated from that location.
The main reduction of this dislocation is that you consider, at that time, if the dislocation is old and already degenerated, you should reduce it as soon as possible according to its position. However, this is not part of the entire treatment; then, one should avoid making assumptions about it.
For the case where the dislocation is recent and one of the four types of dislocation, you should reduce it by returning the joint to its position and extending it inward and outward, then manipulate it to the right and left sides, as it will naturally return with force and does not require further treatment.
Moreover, if it does not return in this manner, you should prepare a strong counterpoise and extend the thigh from that side, or on its hands, or with linseed which is soaked over the thigh above the buttock; another minister should extend his hand from above to ensure his hands touch the tickly parts of the patient; then apply strong linseed paste to the back of the knee, and have another minister press the extremity of the linseed.
And it is the extension from the root inside the angle to the part of the fork, or from the back part to the side of the thigh.
And it is the extension of all those in one go, so that the patient's leg is elevated from his body and hangs down dangling.
And this type of extension is common to all four.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be good. Otherwise, for every type, what I say about its own treatment is necessary.
In the reduction of its own, when dislocated, and the dislocation is on the inner parts, ensure that the patient's leg is flexed towards his side; then place the ligaments over the groove of the thigh in that area where it lies between the head of the femur and the location where the angle is placed; and extend the ligament from above the part of the angle to the next part of the fork.
And then another minister firmly holds the thighs, compresses the thick part of the thigh, and extends to the outer parts with a strong extension, for then it returns to its place.
And this species is of a lighter cure for which this member is reduced compared to other species.
And if you excuse me and do not follow the training for this species of cure, then ensure that both feet of the patient are tightly bound together firmly, loosely over both the ankles and both the toes.
And let the length of each be the quantity of four fingers, and let the patient's leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then let the patient be suspended over the stake's head, and let the hand hold it; and let the length from the ground be the quantity of two braces.
And command a strong youth to hold down the patient's thigh, and let another strong youth suspend the patient, while the other holds down the thigh with force, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
And in the reduction itself, when dislocation is at the outer part, ensure that the patient lies on his side according to what we have recommended for the lying position; and strengthen the bandage over the affected leg and around its foot.
And then two poles are placed aside, one on the foot and the other on the head; and then poles adjusted in the middle of the fire laid over which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not burned because the pole is between the thighs of the patient, for you must allow the extension from the parts left undone to be felt.
And then each minister extends to his part, and the doctor with his hand equalizes the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is good for the leg to be extended slightly, and this is according to this method as narrated for the foot; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the injured part, and then draws that with the other hand, and at that drawing, he extends the leg from the parts left to the front part.
If the dislocation is behind the parts, then do not extend the patient's legs, but elevate them above the ground; you should place them on a hard surface as if the width of the leg were dislocated outward, according to what we have said about its positioning between two widths; and their face and limbs should be extended, as we have said, without much difficulty.
A compress should be applied on the area where the joint is displaced.
So, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and you notice the sign of the joint's reduction, which is that you can extend both legs and when you test them they equalize and the leg returns to its normal position — then you place two cushions and wrap tightly with bandages so that the width is not altered in any part; and adhere the limb to rest for three or four days.
Then remove the bandage, and compare one leg with the other.
When you see equal signs in the wound, then know that the dislocation is already closed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of swelling, then return and apply a plaster to that area, and extend it according to your original structure, and leave it for three days.
Then remove that, and let it gradually heal over each part for some days until it is comfortable.
Capitol..xxxii.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outward parts, to the inward parts, and to the back parts more than to the front parts; and it is not dislocated to the front parts.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you command the patient to apply their hand to their waist; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all manners of it is that you make a direct incision in the malade, essay that it already extends its leg over this; attach your two fingers to the face of it, and place his foot between your feet; then make attach your sole against the palms of yours, and keep those between your named fingers against the sole of him with strength, and another minister extend his leg so that the sole returns to its place.
And the sign of its submission is that its leg adheres to the thigh lightly without effort.
And then apply that and attach the leg to the thigh, and place those occasionally with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove those.
And do not use the malade for ambulation for some days until it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it is excused to you, then administer strong extension to the limbs of which in the cure of the hip the preceding narrative was narrated.
Capitol.xxxiii.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes reduced by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the complement, and the dislocation of the ankle is performed either internally or externally.
And the sign of the dislocation of the ankle is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
More the treatment of the reduction of the ankle is by gentle restoration, and it is that it be extended with ease and returned to its normal position.
More in the treatment of the ankle when it is dislocated according to the complement, make sure you do it right, and have a strong and brave assistant behind the foot at the midpoint.
And then place your right hand on the top part of the foot, and your left hand on the bottom part below the foot at the location of the heel.
And then draw the foot to your right hand, and toward the left for two passes, and extend the left; and push the foot on the right should lower the leg thereby.
And do this for two passes as we have counted.
And then push the foot towards the third pass on the leg, and you are at the knee.
Then if it returns in one or two passes according to this manner, and if you can hold the foot still, that is good; otherwise, then iterate the operation, for if it does not return.
But if reduction of the foot is forbidden as we have advised, then let the patient lie on his side on the ground, and place a firm pillow under him, so that it lies between the patient's thighs, and you have wrapped a cloth around him to prevent the foot from slipping.
And then the minister hold his thighs, and another minister extend the foot with his hands or with cloth tied around the leg; and then each minister extends himself against the length of his part, and the pillow is placed between the patient's thighs holding him, so that the body of the patient is not pulled to the sides as you extend your length.
And then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, while another assists by holding the healthy thigh at the points left, so that the dislocation returns easily.
And then as the dislocation returns and its reduction is declared to be healthy, then apply a strong paste there, and bind the foot with lint to the leg.
And take care to avoid the nerves behind the talus, for the lint over that area should not be strong or severely irritate them.
And leave it there for two or three days; and if the lint becomes moist, replace it.
And remove it on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. But if it is necessary to walk before that time, be sure that the dislocation has not become dissolved and corrupted, and that it does not receive treatment.
And if an abscess occurs at that site, then take care to administer in the extraction of it what we have described in our narrative before, in another location for treatment and fomentation, until it is ripe.
Chapter 34.
For the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
First, ensure that what is dislocated can be reduced with slight extension; and what is not difficult, beforehand is easy.
But if the dislocation is in several bones of the big toe, then you must place the foot on a slightly elevated surface or on a table, and keep it thus as if walking.
Then stand on your feet, and place your foot over the area where the joints are located. Gently press that part with your foot to encourage it to return to its proper position, and observe if it has been reduced and no prominence appears in the area.
Then place the abdomen of the foot on a table that can accommodate the entire foot, which has two supports. Gently maintain this structure firmly for three days; then remove it and allow the foot to rest by walking for several days until it strengthens and its recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Of the types of displacement that occur from injury or fracture or any combination thereof.
When any of these causes occur, and you are involved in the cure of that and its restoration, mishaps may lead to death.
And for this reason, you must not presume in the cure of it; but rather, one who is wise in the art, long experienced, merciful, and pious; who waits, not rash, nor presumptuous; and who uses at the beginning of medicines that cause blisters only temporarily, leaving the disease to divine judgment, but to those to whom you hope for a cure, for alleviation of the illness, in which some hope appears. And then strive to perform the reduction at the beginning of the cause, before blisters occur.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, proceed to administer treatment that causes blisters only and heals the injury with what is appropriate for it from drying ointments.
And if dislocation is accompanied by fracture and accidents in the separated bones, then you must realign their joints, doing so as we have said in the simple ailments that precede here in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear according to which my preaching precedes you; for this is better to you from your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true conscience those in your time. Thus you finish the words of Albucasim perfectly of which may God bless. Amen.
